worked on this English-Dholuo Dictionary. Among them are my informants most of whom have died and | acknowledge them posthumously with reverence. These are Mama Rebecca Odhoch, who was a very special informant, Jaduong' Paul Mboya wuon Akoko,

Jaduong' Simeon Nyende wuon Arua, Jaduong' Oselu Otula, his wife Mama Mandera, Jaduong'Manael Awuor, Jaduong'Christopher Obaje, Mama Paulina Obaje, Mama Mikal Odera, Jaduong' James Opiyo, Jaduong' John Ogodo and Mikaye Lydia Osoro. My special acknowledgement and gratitude go to the late Meshak Ogonji of Sakwa Nyabenge who sat with us late into the night and explained to us the meaning of some of the words and phrases | have included in this dictionary . | also acknowledge Mama Filister Adongo, Jaduong' Oyucho, Mama Mary Oburu and Jaduong'Jorim Obong' who have been my consultants for the last eighteen years, my own parents: my mother Patricia Abuya and my father Blastus Akumu: these two have continued to be my consultants and valuable teachers, Omondi Byron, John and Joyce Humphries for their assistance, especially John who put the dictionary in a diskette in the United Kingdom. Although not a Luo speaker, John did a wonderful job. My acknowledgement also goes to Dr. (Father) Robert Moore who was my colleague at the Institute of African Studies, University of Nairobi who showed me the first Nilotic Dictionary published in 1919 which greatly inspired me, authors of the Luo grammar, vocubulary books and Dictionaries, especially to Dr. Blount and Elise P. Blount whose introduction to his unpublished Luo-English Dictionary | have used here, Mr. R. L. Starfford, the Mill Hill Fathers, Prof. Kokwaro for his Dholuo-English Botanic Dictionary etc., and to those whose names | have not mentioned here, but who in one way

oranother have given me assistance with this Dictionary. Erokamano Maduong' —a big thank you! l offer special acknowledgement to Janet Ochola Ogur, Mrs. J. A. Awinda, Ms. Irene Dulo andthe late Mrs. Grace Ng'uono Ger who typed and retyped the work and lastly to Ms. Janet Achieng' Ogam who has spent hours working with computer on this Dictionary.

Lastly, |express my profound gratitude to my children Peter, the late Jos, Alice, Adhiambo and Suzanne who listed the Luo words alphabetically as | brought them from my field trips and to my husband James for his constant support and encouragement. Asenath Bole Odaga, Kisumu, January, 1996

A number of African Languages received first serious attention in writing from explorers, adventurers, missionaries and merchants: all alien to Africa and to the languages they sought to write about. The control of the development of the study of Africa n Languages by , foreigners became institutionalized during the coloni al era. That is ' why the production of a bilingual dictionary like this one effected by an indigenous “missionary” like Bole Odagais an event of fulfillment. There is a gaping lack of reference materials for the study of, and research on, African languages so that effort such as this is indeed a path-clearing accomplishment. This dictionary will be a vital item to anybo dy interested in knowing, studying or researching on Dholuo, and to the Luo as a people as well as to any researcher interested in studying the languages of ethnic groups neighbouring the Luo. In a sense therefore, although the English- Dholuo dictionary could at first sight be seen to be of immediate help only to those who are seeking access into Kenyan Dholuo through English, when viewed more as a reference material, the dictionary will be crucia l in comparative study and understanding of other East and Central African peoples and their languages. After all there has been a socio-linguistic excha nge amongst the speakers of languages of Western Kenya with some languages actually genetically related and Dholuo also relate s to many other Languages beyond the Kenyan boarders. There fore for a quick word-check, this dictionary will seem to be of help to only those users with competence in English or Dholuo or both, but, for those who will want to use it as a study instrument, it is important to note the significant interaction between Dholuo and neigh bouring languages like Ekegusii, the Kalenjin language groups and the Luhya language groups of Western Kenya. Apart from the fact that speakers of Kenyan Dholuo spill over the boarders of Kenya into Uganda and Tanzania, the language is linked to many language groups in Uganda, Sudan, Ethiopia, the Central African Republ ic, Zaire and Chad. Bole Odaga's method of presentation makes the dictionary

usable by a wide range of people. The presentation is simple with more than one Dholuo word given for every English word. Where

word forwordis viewed as inadequate, brief descriptive and illustrative

examples are given to bring out the meaning of an English word. To avoid rendering the dictionary too complex for general users, the linguistic explanations about Dholuo are summarized in the introduction but even there they are presented simply. In summary, Bole Odaga has presented us with what can be referred to as a worthy pioneer work and has laboured for many years to break for us the ground on which to build. And in building we must echo even ifwith a slight adaptation Ludwig Krapf's wisdom expressed in his introduction to his Kiswahili Dictionary of 1982 that a people's language must be approached with an open and philosophical mind in awareness that a language always speaks to us the psychological stance of a community and therefore always reflects the community's thoughts about itself, animals and other natural phenomena and about metaphysical realms like God, life and death. We must feel obliged to accept that the research for the best manner of approach is a challenge to us, not again to Bole Odaga who has so kindly given us a start. Owen O. McOnyango Department of African Languages Maseno University College

INTRODUCTION The Luo people live in Western Kenya, Nyanza Province around Lake Victoria which is known to themas Nam Lolwe. The lake links the three East African countries of Uganda, Tanzania and Kenya. The Luo are also found in Uganda Tanzania and in Zaire as well.

J-

2

Dholuo is part of the Western Nilotic languages which belong to Eastern Sudanic language groups, and forms part of the Chari-Nile branch of Nilo-Saharan languages. These make up one of the main language groups in Africa. (Greenberg, 1966) Dholuo spoken in Kenya has few linguistic differences and almost negligible dialectical variations. Thus, Dholuo spoken in parts of Siaya, that is in Alego, Ugenya and Gem have slight variations from that which is spoken in Bondo which is also in Siaya and also from that spoken by the Luos of Kisumu District. There are fur slight the variation r s in Southern Nyanza with Dholuo spoken in Kisumu and parts of Siaya District, mainly in Bondo Division and with that which is spoken in Kisumu District. The slight differences are in speech which is faster in Kisumu District and Bondo Division than it is in Southern Nyanza, Ugenya, Alego and Gem. The difference in vocabulary is slight e.g. in Alego cow dung is sometime wuoyo while in other areas it is owuoyo. lt is also worth noticing here that Dholuo has no uniform orthography. Some of the words such as hu, u, fu — puff adder or like in fuok, huok, wuok or fulu, ulu, hulu, hu iyo, uyo, uno, huno, have beginnings and middles with vowels and syllables that are interchangeable. This is also the case with wand uo e.g. mwolo and the same word is written as muolo— kindness ng'wono, ng'uon-o mercy. Most people | spoke to seem to feel that standardised orthography would interfere with the richness of the language. Dholuo is tonal and shouldbe phonetically written. Some words e.g, moko has several meanings. When written as moko the same word also means others, such as other things, gik moko. |. Phonemes. Luo has 9 vowel phonemes and 21 consonant phonemes A. li, Morphophonemics It is important to note the following while using the final

consonant alteration, vowel harmony and vowel elision.

A. Final Consonant Alteration Csy ich iya r=C oguro oguch mama m= mb muma mumbe m = mb bam/em bambe/embe l= nd kul kunde g=nd gul gunde w=p pap pawe d=t kit kido g=k opuk opugni = nj pany panje p=nj piny pinje

my stomach my mother's back bibles thighs cow bomas riversides fields colour this tortoise mortars countries

B. Vowel Harmony Morphemes with open root vowels (essentially vowels of stressed syllables) require open vowels in affixes. Closed root vowels will also require closed vowels in affixes. e =e “his, her, its” kom ‘chair’; kombe “chair-his” tong’ “eggs”; tonge “eggs-his/her”

ST

ngg

Some affixes trigger vowel harmony in root morphemes. guok “dog”; guoge “dogs, pl.” amosi, / greet you - amose - “I greet him/her”

/C. Vowel Elision When two vowels are separated by word or morpheme

boundaries, the first vowel is dropped. wuoche wuoch=oti “shoe-old” palazodik > pal¢odik “knife-dull” kazibiro > kibiro “if you come”

Thus,entries may appear with or without final vowels, depending on context. Affixation Prefixes nya- (sg.) nyi- (pl.); diminutive; young hyaruath young bull nyiruedhi (pl) ja(sg.) Jo (pl) agentive person jawuoth, traveller, jowuoth- travellers raperson's trait ragwel, bow legged person rainstrument prefix ragol - comb radenotes colour rateng’ - black marelative marker. You will note that many adjectives in Luo are made up of relative clauses introduced by ma- e.g., ng’at mariek man/person who is clever, a clever man, mawinjo, who hears. minominalizing prefix. miganga, thief.

This is the first English-Dholuo C whosefirstlanguageis Luo. Anda be complete, it is a wonderful pioi source for students of African languages, particuarly for those studying Nilotic languages. To the Luo this is a valuable book, for after all a language is a carrier of a peoples values and 1 culture. The author, Bole Odaga is a well known writs: wo?"Nag written and done intensive research on Kalenjin, Ginam, :osii, o

1.

Kuria and Luo oral literature and traditions. Her cther wo: pain

Dholuo are: Poko Nyar Migumba, Ogilo Nungo Piny Kirom, The

Luo Sayings, Nyamgondho wuod Ombare and Dholuo--- English — Dictionaryamong others. Bole has also written novels, chidren's fiction and books on oral literature such as Yesterday's Today: The Study of Oral Literature, Kenyan Folk Tales, Simbi Nyaima - aplay in English, Endless Road, Riana etc. Bole lives in Kisumu where the family runs Thu Tinda Bookshop and Lake Publishers & Enterprises Ltd.
